From davisbarrett at gmail.com  Sat Jan 10 13:05:37 2009
From: davisbarrett at gmail.com (JD Barrett)
Date: Sat, 10 Jan 2009 13:05:37 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Homeschool & Edubuntu [cross-posted]
Message-ID: <7802bcd40901101305v54ec4384pfae028ce6323d668@mail.gmail.com>

Hey all,

I work with a group of homeschooling parents interested in the use of
Edubuntu (myself included).  Generally we are local to the Portland, Oregon
area but there are members beyond that.  Initially we started working on an
article for a linux e-mag (which we're still working on) but it's kind of
grown from that.

Eons ago, when rocks were soft and dirt was new, I had heard that the school
systems of Belgium, Denmark, and the Netherlands were exploring the use of
Linux as a completely integrated system between students, teachers, and
parents.  A more recent search digs up no further information, so we're kind
of going it alone at this point.

If you have any links, information, or wish to participate in the yahoo
group directly please either respond to this list or feel free to join us at
www.groups.yahoo.com/homeschoolfoss to help out.  This group is not just
open to Portland, Oregon area homeschoolers, or even homeschoolers.  If you
are comfortable, please post this notice (or one of your writing) in other
groups that you feel might find it useful.

I find it interesting the correlation between the ethos of the Linux
community and the ethos of the Homeschooling community.  Some might
mistakenly believe them to be opposite forces, assuming Linux to be liberal
and Homeschoolers to be opposite (or vice versa), but a closer examination
will show that their core goals are quite similar.

Cheers,
Jimmy Barrett

JD Barrett wrote:
> [[snip]

There was/is a project (Knoppix / Debian based) called FreeDuc. They
produced two live CDs, the first of which was aimed at middle school --
high school students, and the second of which was aimed at elementary
school students. I downloaded the elementary one a few days ago. It
comes up with a European keymapping and there's no easy way to override
that without doing a Knoppix remaster. It does boot, however, at which
point you have an XFCE desktop with all the menus in French.

The one for older students is a bit more usable, although dated. I
checked out Edubuntu, but it is no longer distributed as a single
LiveCD. You have to install a Ubuntu desktop and then an Edubuntu
add-on. openSUSE also has an education add-on; they either just released
it or are in the final release candidate stage of it.

And, of course, there is the collection of software on the OLPC XO,
which is Fedora-based. In short, there is no shortage of open source
educational software.

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Sat Jan 10 21:01:47 2009
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason Martin)
Date: Sat, 10 Jan 2009 21:01:47 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Trying to learn low level Linux programming for cs201...
In-Reply-To: <20090110050425.GH2425@sentinelchicken.org>
References: <1231541294.15092.4.camel@eagle.robinson-west.com>
	<4967D8EF.8070808@fredjame.cnc.net>
	<20090109231222.GE2425@sentinelchicken.org>
	<49680D42.1020803@fredjame.cnc.net>
	<20090110050425.GH2425@sentinelchicken.org>
Message-ID: <c29537840901102101v36bd1291s2918e5db54c27105@mail.gmail.com>

On Fri, Jan 9, 2009 at 9:04 PM, Tim <tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org> wrote:
> Hi Fred,
>
>> Could you elaborate, please, or point to such?
>> Regards
>> Fred James
>>
>> PS:  Sorry - I should narrow the scope a bit - I meant the "unsafe" part
>> - thank you.
>
> Sure.  I guess a good file-related example would be fscanf().  If you
> try to read in strings or other variable length arguments without
> putting an explicit length limiter in there, you're essentially
> guaranteeing yourself a buffer overflow.
>
> Another example might be fprintf() which, while typically safer, is
> dangerous to include user-supplied values in the format string itself.
> For instance, doing something like:
>  fprintf(FILE_pointer, user_supplied_string);
>
> Can allow for arbitrary code execution amongst other things.  Of course
> this issue isn't limited to fprintf()...  All of the printf() family
> exhibit this.
>
> Finally, consider popen() which returns a FILE object pointing to a
> stream.  It executes '/bin/sh -c ...' with your input string.  If any
> user-supplied values slip into that string, you're in big trouble.  Even
> if this wasn't an issue, popen() and it's friend system() have a
> horrible interface in that they don't return good information about
> whether or not your external process failed.  If you want to pipe data
> to your sub process, forget trying to get anything back on it's stdout.
>
> Other examples of poor wrapper function design (temporary file creation,
> many bad string functions) move further away from FILE-specific stuff,
> but one often finds that using just some of these higher-level
> interfaces is hard.  You often have to either just write it yourself at
> the low level or give in and use all of libc's bad wrappers.
>
> In the end, these interfaces can be used safely of course, if you know
> what you're doing.  A better interface can of course be used unsafely if
> you try hard enough.  To distinguish between the two, you should ask the
> question:
>  "Is the most obvious way to do a thing also the safe way of doing it?"

The best resource on this topic I've seen is David Wheeler's free book
"Secure Programming for Linux and Unix HOWTO"
(http://www.dwheeler.com/secure-programs/).

Cheers,
Jason


From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sun Jan 11 14:03:27 2009
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sun, 11 Jan 2009 14:03:27 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Simulators/Emulators/VMs for Palm and Android
Message-ID: <20090111220327.GA17612@gate.kl-ic.com>

My wife the doctor spends a lot of time thinking about her patients,
and insufficient time thinking about keys, pagers, cell phones, etc.
There are some medical apps that she would like to run that store
data on a Palm PDA.  In time, I expect there will be medical apps 
that she can run on Android.  However, I also expect she will lose 
the physical devices from time to time.  It would be bad if she had
to buy new apps and configure them each time she did so.  It would
be disasterous if there was patient data on them.  

An alternative is to run the apps on a virtual machine running on
her (well secured and not going anywhere) Linux desktop.  I know
there is an emulator for the Google Android; perhaps there are
decent emulators for the Palm.  I don't think she needs Garnet
(the latest version of the Palm OS) to run the Palm apps she wants. 

Does anybody have experience or informed opinions about these
emulators?  I'm particularly interested in experience with 
running Palm apps in these environments, as there are a lot of
them out there.

Keith

From ianburrell at gmail.com  Mon Jan 12 12:52:04 2009
From: ianburrell at gmail.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Mon, 12 Jan 2009 12:52:04 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Simulators/Emulators/VMs for Palm and Android
In-Reply-To: <20090111220327.GA17612@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20090111220327.GA17612@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <d91f09cd0901121252u5175ab8bs83a3d19410a91a05@mail.gmail.com>

On Sun, Jan 11, 2009 at 2:03 PM, Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com> wrote:
> My wife the doctor spends a lot of time thinking about her patients,
> and insufficient time thinking about keys, pagers, cell phones, etc.

> data on a Palm PDA.  In time, I expect there will be medical apps

> the physical devices from time to time.  It would be bad if she had

> be disasterous if there was patient data on them.
>
> An alternative is to run the apps on a virtual machine running on
> her (well secured and not going anywhere) Linux desktop.  I know
> there is an emulator for the Google Android; perhaps there are
> decent emulators for the Palm.  I don't think she needs Garnet
> (the latest version of the Palm OS) to run the Palm apps she wants.
>
> Does anybody have experience or informed opinions about these
> emulators?  I'm particularly interested in experience with
> running Palm apps in these environments, as there are a lot of
> them out there.
>

There is the Palm OS Emulator [1]. It is open source but requires ROM
images from Palm. Getting the ROM images requires being signed up for
the free developer program. It can only emulate the old 68K devices
(Palm OS 4 and earlier) and won't run Palm OS 5 only programs. There
is a Palm OS Simulator which simulates Palm OS 5 devices but it only
runs on Windows.

There is also the Garnet VM from Access (which bought PalmSource).
There is an older Garnet VM Compatibility Kit [2] which runs on normal
Linux. I think it also requires a developer account to download. They
have also released the Garnet VM for the Nokia internet tablets [3].

 - Ian

1: http://sourceforge.net/projects/pose/
2: http://www.accessdevnet.com/index.php/ACCESS-Garnet-VM-Compatibility-Kit-Downloads/View-category.html
3: http://www.access-company.com/products/gvm/

From tcslighter at gmail.com  Mon Jan 12 16:31:30 2009
From: tcslighter at gmail.com (Tim Slighter)
Date: Mon, 12 Jan 2009 16:31:30 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] denyhosts not blocking some ssh attempts
In-Reply-To: <496BC256.2030802@seitzassoc.com>
References: <496BC256.2030802@seitzassoc.com>
Message-ID: <496BE0E2.3060201@gmail.com>

I don't know but my approach has always been from the opposite side of 
the spectrum, I only use /etc/hosts.allow with an implicit deny for any 
TCP/UDP based inbound connection.  This approach is not always practical 
when dealing with thousands or millions of potential connecting 
hosts/networks but if it is your home system, save yourself all the 
extra work and use /etc/hosts.allow instead

> each end. The desktop would go into the final recepticle, whcih would be the
> wall outlet. At some point I'll fix it up with a junction box, but wondered
> if the above would work as a hack.
>   

It should. Although, I've never tried to wire a plug. I'd put 
receptacles on both ends and use factory made patch cables.

If you use a receptacle from a system like Leviton's, it will snap into 
a cover plate when you get around to putting in the box. It doesn't have 
to be a box, either. There are drywall brackets designed for low voltage 
applications that provide the screw holes for cover plates without a box 
in the back.

It's been a few years since I bought any, but Home Depot used to carry 
all the stuff.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens
www.dicksteffens.com

On Mon, Jan 12, 2009 at 18:05, Roderick A. Anderson
<raanders at cyber-office.net> wrote:
> Going with the deny hosts thread I reread some info I found on blocking
> sshd DoS attacks using iptables.
>
> Before actually deploying them I thought it might be a good idea to test
> on my workstation rather than on the servers 35 miles away.  :-/
>
> Problem is: How do I flood my system with ssh connections?
>
> Is there a neat trick to do this just using ssh or do I need a script?
> (Quick search didn't pick up anything obvious.  Wrong terms?  Probably!)

since the daemon and hosts.allow/deny are all triggered by any kind of
connection, you could do something like

netcat -z localhost 22

in a while or for loop from the command line.

This is common in anti-spam measures, such as greylisting 
(http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greylisting).  Check the headers and you 
should see something like:

X-Greylist: delayed 1801 seconds by postgrey-1.27 at www.dnaedge.com; Tue, 13 Jan 2009 09:07:49 CST

J

vlc media player to play a DVD using an external DVD burner I

solve.  The firefox version on it is 2.0.0.14, which doesn't 
support the media connectivity plugin.  I bought crossover 
linux and installed IE 6, but it installed it in a win98 
bottle which won't support a decent release of windows media 
player.  Crossover is a pain to work with, it has to be 
launched from the command line because of the custom window 
manager's lack of support for custom shortcuts.  I guess 
the system is just plain old and not very upgradable.
I know that the solid state drive is only 20 gigs, but that
should be plenty of room for a more up to date Linux system.

I'm not sure what all the repositories are for debian, but I
seem to have added the standard suspects by adding a records
that points to the osu open source lab site.  I couldn't find
libdvdcss2, so I finally cheated and downloaded it directly
opting to install it with dpkg instead of apt-get.  

I wish I could add a shortcut to vlc media player to the window 
manager.  Uge!  

Anyone else have experience with the Asus Eee 900 running Linux?

I seem to have the standard C and C++ tools installed for 
programming, which surprisingly appear to be fairly current.

From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Wed Jan 14 08:11:51 2009
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Wed, 14 Jan 2009 08:11:51 -0800

In-Reply-To: <1231922501.11892.9.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
References: <1231922501.11892.9.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <20090114161151.GC2383@sentinelchicken.org>

On Wed, Jan 14, 2009 at 12:41:41AM -0800, Someone wrote:
> Is it possible to disallow the use of firefox by certain users.
> 
> For example:
> 
> /home
> 
> /home/foo /home/bar /home/admin /home/goo
> 
> Where foo, bar, and admin should be allowed to use firefox but
> goo should not.

Well, you could add the users you want to be privileged to a "firefox"
group, and then set the permissions on all applicable binaries to
"rwxr-x---" and set those binaries' groups to "firefox".

tim

I guess the question becomes, how smart are the users?  since i can
download my own copy of firefox and call the binary "vi", i'm not sure
there's any way of actually preventing someone even mildly interested
in using it from doing so.

SELinux or apparmor might be the best method after all, next to not
giving them account, or not giving them write access (or an extremely
tiny disk quota).

On Wed, Jan 14, 2009 at 10:49 PM, Russell Senior
<russell at personaltelco.net>wrote:

> >>>>> "Matt" == Matt McKenzie <lnxknight at gmail.com> writes:
>
> Matt> I would add a +1 to ditch the tweaked Xandros and put EEEBuntu
> Matt> (Ubuntu) or EEEDora (Fedora) on it.  Customize to taste, stir,
> Matt> and serve. :)
>
> The biggest upside to leaving Xandros on is that it boots fast.  I
> installed EEEBuntu on an SD card but it takes about 4 minutes to get
> me to a prompt.  Xandros is usable in about 30 seconds.
>
>
>

Well that is part of the problem I think, Ubuntu is on the SD card, Xandros
is on the internal flash(?) or hdd, which I would imagine has a faster bus
speed than the SD port.
You might try using a faster capable SD card (still limited by the bus
though), but why not replace Xandros on the internal flash with Ubuntu?

It is similar to comparing a LiveCD distro to one installed on the HDD.  The
access time is going to be longer.

I saw someone at the OSCON last year with EEEDora on the Atom powered EEEPC,
he had customized it and got it to boot very quickly, I think it was around
15-20 seconds.

The issue isn't the distro, it is the media it is installed on.

At any rate I have no first hand experience with these netbooks yet but it
is on my "list", though I have tinkered with them a few times.

In-Reply-To: <b27ba6870901150908i17c0dad5j585bb96abf3635e0@mail.gmail.com>
References: <b27ba6870901150908i17c0dad5j585bb96abf3635e0@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20090115171448.GA2379@sentinelchicken.org>

> A friend picked up a really cool looking Mac from Freegeek
> for public use in his cafe.  It's running Ubuntu, but unless we
> can find a Flash player for the Power PC architecture and
> Ubuntu O.S., he's going to have to shell out for MacOS.
> 
> Does anyone on this list have any pointers to a Linux/PPC
> Flash player?

I can think of two possibilities:

1. See if gnash compiles on PPC (or has precompiled packages).  I would
   be surprised at this point if it didn't support PPC, but I have been
   surprised before.

2. Try to run the stock i386 flash from Adobe under Qemu.  No, I'm not
   talking about running a full VM.  Qemu can also do single-process
   instruction translation.  I've heard of people running i386 windows
   processes under Wine under Qemu on PPC.  It's insane that it works,
   but apparently it does. YMMV.

Good luck,
tim

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Thu Jan 15 11:45:48 2009
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Thu, 15 Jan 2009 11:45:48 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] eee pc box
Message-ID: <20090115194548.GA1943@gate.kl-ic.com>

I bought an EEE PC *BOX* for my wife to use with (sigh) Windows XP
and Dragon Naturally Speaking 10.  It arrived a yesterday.  Before
I let it succumb to the nexus of evil, I booted it to an Ubuntu
Live CD on an external USB CDrom drive.  I am currently typing
this in through an xterm on running on that distro, through
ssh to my mail server.

The box is a repackaged version of the N270 Atom hardware that
makes up the EEE PC micronotebook.  It has a 160GB Seagate SATA
drive preinstalled with (gag again) WinXP home.  This was $330
from Amazon (shipped from Sparks NV) and arrived in 2.5 days
(ordered sunday night).  There are also versions of the same
B202 machine that come with something called LinuxEZOS and cost
$300.  

The box is about the size of a hardback novel, has a DVI port with
an add-on HD15 VGA adapter, 4 USB ports, a GBit Ethernet port, an
SD memory card port, some audio ports,  and a N wireless antenna
connector.  It is powered by an external AC adapter that draws 1W
standby, 14 watts running, probably lots more when doing disk access
while computing its little brains out.

It comes with a keyboard and mouse, and with a mounting plate to
attach it to the back of a flatscreen.

One surprising feature is that it has a miniature version of Linux
running from ROM:  Something called Expressgate powered by splashtop
from a place called DeviceVM.  From poweron, one can click icons on
the splash page to launch a stripped down web browser (resembling
firefox), games, and other web-based stuff.  So if you need a
browser 5 seconds from poweron, this is your baby.  The video 
configurations are limited.

What wireless card?  LSPCI shows:
   Network controller: RaLink Unknown device 0781 
for what I presume is the wireless adapter.  On the side of the
box are these device descriptions:
  AzureWave     FCC id
    AW-NE766    VQF-RT2700E
    AW-NE771    PPD-AR5B91

More to learn.  Linux compatability unknown.  If I use the wireless
at all, it will be as an access point - if possible.  If not, I will
turn it off.

What video display?   /var/log/Xorg.0.log shows an Intel 945GME Express
(or possibly a 943/940GML ) chipset.  The drivers from lsmod look like
the /might/ be i915 and intel_agp ... more investigation needed.  I have
a 1400x1050 LCD display that came up as 1024x768.  If I installed it
for real, I'm pretty sure I could match the LCD.  The Splashtop only
offers a choice of 7 resolutions, but the 1440x1050 choice looks OK.
WinXP connects to the LCD at 1400x1050.

Audio?  LSPCI says:
     Audio device: Intel Corporation 82801G (ICH7 Family)  \
            High Definition Audio Controller (rev 02)

I don't know how well that works, I haven't hooked it up.

So, I now reboot back to XP and prepare the speech recognition app.  
his will either not run on the perhaps-slow Atom, or it will only
run under native XP, or it might run under VMWare or VirtualBox. 
I don't think I can run Xen on the Atom, as it appears to lack the
virtualization instructions needed.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From creswick at gmail.com  Thu Jan 15 16:09:50 2009
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Thu, 15 Jan 2009 16:09:50 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Construct all logic using NOR...
In-Reply-To: <1232057981.3069.11.camel@eagle.robinson-west.com>
References: <1232057981.3069.11.camel@eagle.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <c834ec80901151609g5a96c134p6341aa5d0ef3d1f1@mail.gmail.com>

On Thu, Jan 15, 2009 at 2:19 PM, Michael Robinson
<plug_1 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
> My Hein book for PSU Discrete Stuctures II does not teach at all how I
> am supposed to construct all connectives from NOR.  For example, how
> do I come up with OR?  How about AND?  There is NOT and IMPLIES

Truth Tables and Karnaugh Maps
(http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karnaugh_map) are the primary tools I
always used when working on things like this.

Don't forget that you can split inputs (signals, wires, copy
variables, whatever you want to call them) and you can use True and
False constants to make a function (gate) that takes two inputs only
require 1.

--Rogan


On Thu, Jan 15, 2009 at 2:19 PM, Michael Robinson
<plug_1 at robinson-west.com> wrote:

> am supposed to construct all connectives from NOR.  For example, how
> do I come up with OR?  How about AND?  There is NOT and IMPLIES as
> well.  I've looked up the answers, but all I find are the answers
> which doesn't do me any good.  I need to learn the method of showing
> that NOR is enough to represent everything.

From plug_1 at robinson-west.com  Thu Jan 15 23:01:29 2009
From: plug_1 at robinson-west.com (Michael Robinson)
Date: Thu, 15 Jan 2009 23:01:29 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Linux and MS Windows, the future...
Message-ID: <1232089289.3601.45.camel@eagle.robinson-west.com>

ReactOS may be based on 1.6 million lines of code, but it still 
seems incredibly small compared to the system it is trying to 
replace.  That isn't saying much though because ReactOS will 
undoubtedly get bigger and possibly much bigger as it stabilizes.

Where is Linux going in the future?  ReactOS developers claim 
that Linux+WINE is not the best solution.  Maybe they are right,
maybe the best solution is to get more vendors to write native
Linux applications.  Speed wise, native applications are better
than anything that has to be run in a virtual or para virtual
environment.  On the other hand, not having to worry about
legacy support because virtualization is available can keep
Linux from bloating badly.  The trouble of setting up older
Linux distributions in virtual environments to support legacy
Linux apps is that most X installations assume you will be
accessing email, etcetera.  Evolution seems to have become
highly integrated into Gnome.  I guess there's a push to be
able to have one's email application etcetera pop up 
automatically, but there's a lot of work to do.  Firefox 
doesn't support plugins as well on Linux as it does in 
Windows.  I rarely ask Firefox to install something 
automatically where I don't have to install manually.  
Linux preinstalled on new computers still seems to be 
fairly rare and the Asus Eee PC I gave my nephew should 
probably be redone with Ubuntu 8.  I'm afraid that the 
video drivers are proprietary though.

My desktop is completely Linux based.  I have a registered copy of
VMware Workstation 6, but I mostly use it to monitor the progress
of the ReactOS project.  For running games, I could have a Windows
virtual machine, but I don't particularly want to go there.

Maybe Microsoft should do something Earth shattering, like distribute
it's own Linux distribution with a native version of Internet Explorer
and other popular programs for free.  Maybe Microsoft should take
notice of the WINE project and offer a commercial product that emulates
WIN32k on top of Linux even better.  Maybe Microsoft and other companies
that develop for Windows should change how drivers are done so that they
will work equally well in Linux as they will in Windows.  If I were
running Microsoft, I'd refocus the company to work on getting an open
source and free version of Windows XP out and I'd change the company's
focus to making Windows applications run on Linux.  I'd say let's slash
the prices, let's get back to writing small programs, let's take the
kitchen sink out of popular applications and see what they can really
do.

What is this Midora or Midori research project that I keep hearing
about?  Supposedly, Microsoft is going to abandon the NT architecture
for something that is designed to work with web based software.  I'm
thinking this will suck for anyone who cares about privacy or
portability, but Microsoft could certainly offer a less bloated OS
if all Microsoft wants to do is support the running of web based apps.

Check your serial port and settings
Exiting with status SYNC_ERROR_BIND
Finished.

   The specified port within jpilot used to work as /dev/tts/USB1. That
desn't work and neither does /dev/ttyUSB1.

   Plugging in the USB cable tells HAL there's a new device, and that's
recorded by /var/log/messages:

Jan 16 08:42:50 salmo kernel: usb 2-1: new full speed USB device using
ohci_hcd and address 69
Jan 16 08:42:50 salmo kernel: usb 2-1: configuration #1 chosen from 1 choice
Jan 16 08:42:50 salmo kernel: visor 2-1:1.0: Handspring Visor / Palm OS
converter detected
Jan 16 08:42:50 salmo kernel: usb 2-1: Handspring Visor / Palm OS converter
now attached to ttyUSB0
Jan 16 08:42:50 salmo kernel: usb 2-1: Handspring Visor / Palm OS converter
now attached to ttyUSB1

   However, when I look for /dev/ttyUSB1 it's not found. With the above
showing the device connected, I cannot find any /dev/ttyUSB and /dev/tts/
contains only 1, 2, 3, and 4, not ttyUSB1. I think this is the cause of the
problem. Where do I look to learn what broke?

Date: Fri, 16 Jan 2009 09:44:56 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] denyhosts not blocking some ssh attempts
In-Reply-To: <alpine.LNX.2.00.0901130619190.22600@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <496BC256.2030802@seitzassoc.com> <496BE0E2.3060201@gmail.com>
	<496BFAF1.6080805@gmail.com>
	<2e42bd9b0901121908l195919afx6d768168e161d7f1@mail.gmail.com>
	<alpine.LNX.2.00.0901130619190.22600@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <8975a95f0901160944j44e387fw9517c9fdfd02fce7@mail.gmail.com>

On Tue, Jan 13, 2009 at 6:24 AM, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>   Allowing only a limited number of user names to connect via ssh works for
> us because there are fewer than a handful of names.

(bit of an aside here...)
If all your legitimate users are allowed to SSH in, what's the point
of specifically allowing users? All other names will be invalid and
login will fail regardless of whether or not they are allowed. If you
create users that have valid shells configured for ftp access or
whatever else, then it becomes something to worry about. I've seen a
lot of people setup SSH allowed-users lists for no good reason. One
good reason would be to allow root to login, but only from certain
IPs. So adding "root at 10.0.0.1". Another good thing to do with this if
you need remote root access is to set "allowrootlogin =
without-password". That somewhat un-intuitively makes it so that root
can login only with authentication mechanisms _other than_ password,
ie - public keys. The net result of those two settings makes it so
that root can only login using a key file from a certain IP. Very
useful, and still secure enough for most purposes.

> For my edification, is
> it impractical to maintain /etc/hosts.allow when there are many users on the
> network?

The number of users doesn't matter so much as the number of IP's they
will be coming from. If you have a known subset of IP's from which all
legitimate access will originate, hosts.allow with an implicit deny
makes sense. If not, it's likely to become a management headache. Or
an "I'm at the pub and they have wifi and my laptop is in the car, but
I can't login and fix this random problem that I just got paged on
here because of my implicit deny rule so I have to go home/to the
office to fix it anyway and now my friday night is totally disrupted
because I didn't really think that through." sort of headache. :D

>   Second question that comes from this discussion is the relative advantages
> of hosts.allow and hosts.deny. It would be nice to have someone clarify the
> differences for me.

In a nutshell, IPs in .deny are not allowed to connect, IPs in .allow
are. That is grossly oversimplified though.

The googley-bear has a ton of info on this, as do the man pages. Do a
search for "hosts.deny hosts.allow" and you'll come up with lots
interesting stuff. A "man hosts.deny" or "man hosts.allow" gives
useful stuff too.


-QH-

   Actually, your answer to the second part of my first question is a better
answer. By specifying ALL: in /etc/hosts.allow there's no need to specify IP
addresses. That would be a major hassle when traveling on business and
connecting from all sorts of strange places -- including the brewpub on
Friday evening. Therefore, the onus for security is on /etc/hosts.deny and
there the default is ALL: unless it's a known username asking to come in.

From rsteff at comcast.net  Fri Jan 16 13:42:32 2009
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Fri, 16 Jan 2009 13:42:32 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] /home/user partition question
Message-ID: <4970FF48.7090601@comcast.net>

Common practice is to have a user directory under /home. Many programs 
assume such a thing, and put stuff there. One of the arguments is that 
it makes backups easier. That's good, except that there are things I've 
kept from old installations that I need to integrate into my current 
installation without messing up other things. So, I can simply replace 
my current /home/rsteff with my old /home/rsteff.

Is it practical to consider creating a separate partition for Documents? 
Or are there programs out there that will want to put installation 
specific things into /home/rsteff/Documents?

If this is practical, then I could consider Documents as the place that 
wouldn't change from distro to distro, should I decide to try loading 
different distros on the machine.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens
www.dicksteffens.com

From heinlein at madboa.com  Fri Jan 16 13:59:50 2009
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Fri, 16 Jan 2009 13:59:50 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] /home/user partition question
In-Reply-To: <4970FF48.7090601@comcast.net>
References: <4970FF48.7090601@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.2.00.0901161352460.32651@guild.galois.com>

On Fri, 16 Jan 2009, Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> Common practice is to have a user directory under /home. Many 
> programs assume such a thing, and put stuff there.

I certainly hope not. User home directories ought to be determined via 
the getpwent() routines, $HOME, or their moral equivalent. Assuming 
/home/$USER is a recipe for a major disaster. Developers doing such a 
thing ought to be banished!

> One of the arguments is that it makes backups easier. That's good, 
> except that there are things I've kept from old installations that I 
> need to integrate into my current installation without messing up 
> other things. So, I can simply replace my current /home/rsteff with 
> my old /home/rsteff.
>
> Is it practical to consider creating a separate partition for 
> Documents? Or are there programs out there that will want to put 
> installation specific things into /home/rsteff/Documents?

Other than Mac OS X, I've never seen an auto-created $HOME/Documents.

Nor have I seen Linux applications that assume the existence of 
~/Documents.

But when you say "separate partition," are saying that you want to 
hard-mount a block device as ~rsteff/Documents?

That doesn't sound like a good idea.

A separate partition for stuff that you're going to carry through 
various upgrades is a good idea, but I wouldn't *mount* it under your 
home directory. I'd mount it as, say, /opt/Documents and then symlink 
to it from your home directory.

Does that make sense?

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/

non-conforming HTML in it.  Likely it
has nothing to do with "access".

>   The problem is apparently with pilot-xfer:
>
> [rshepard at salmo ~/.jpilot]$ pilot-xfer -p /dev/ttyUSB1 -s .
>    Unable to bind to port: /dev/ttyUSB1
>    Please use --help for more information

pilot-xfer is EXTREMELY finicky w/usb devices.  it's what i use, but
if your timing isn't right (down to the split-second) on trying to
sync after you plugin and the device is recognized, it won't work and
that is the error message it will give.  (note, this is only a problem
with the usb treo/visor products--i used it for 8 years with a serial
palm and it was rock solid and there was no timing issue).

i wouldn't judge the pilot-xfer software to be the cause of your
current problem, since it's what's used udner the hood of jpilot and
apparently jpilot is better at handling the timing somehow (how, is
beyond me, since i always felt like it was a kernel<->pilot-xfer
problem, and jpilot's not in the middle of that but rather on top of
the pilot-xfer end)

i really think a reboot might be in order.  i hate to suggest it, but
that's my last input on this one.  good luck!

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Fri Jan 16 18:09:55 2009
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Fri, 16 Jan 2009 18:09:55 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] eee pc box (2)
In-Reply-To: <20090115194548.GA1943@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20090115194548.GA1943@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <20090117020955.GB9430@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Thu, Jan 15, 2009 at 11:45:48AM -0800, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> What video display?   /var/log/Xorg.0.log shows an Intel 945GME Express
> (or possibly a 943/940GML ) chipset.  The drivers from lsmod look like
> the /might/ be i915 and intel_agp ... more investigation needed.  I have
> a 1400x1050 LCD display that came up as 1024x768.  If I installed it
> for real, I'm pretty sure I could match the LCD.  The Splashtop only
> offers a choice of 7 resolutions, but the 1440x1050 choice looks OK.
> WinXP connects to the LCD at 1400x1050.

I installed Ubuntu onto another drive, and configured it.  The 
display still came up at 1024x768.  I found the problem - the
chipset and software think there are two screens, with the second
screen defaulting to 1024x768.  Ubuntu as installed clones the
screens, hence locking them both to the smaller resolution.  

In  System -> Preferences -> Screen Resolution  uncheck "Clone
Screen" and then select the (now separate) laptop window and
set the resolution to "off".  I found the latter necessary to
keep the somewhat wonky driver from occasionally rendering an 
extra and enlarged version of some windows onto the desktop.

There may be other bugs awaiting me in Ubuntu - when I bring the 
eee pc box to the Clinic on Sunday, more experienced Ubuntu users
( Ubusers?  Ubunnies? ) can look for more.

Keith

From dvhoward at teleport.com  Fri Jan 16 18:44:32 2009
From: dvhoward at teleport.com (Douglas Howard)
Date: Fri, 16 Jan 2009 21:44:32 -0500 (EST)
Subject: [PLUG] Construct all logic using NOR...
Message-ID: <23650330.1232160272764.JavaMail.root@mswamui-billy.atl.sa.earthlink.net>

This topic is important for utilizing hardware effectively. You can do everything with NORs or NANDs. 
I found some good links: 

These links spell it out:
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NAND_logic 
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NOR_logic 

These elaborate and are good info:
http://www.kpsec.freeuk.com/gates.htm 
http://www.rbjones.com/rbjpub/logic/log048.htm 
http://www.allaboutcircuits.com/vol_4/chpt_3/9.html 
http://www.antonine-education.co.uk/Electronics_AS/Electronics_Mod2/topic_2_1/using_nand__or_nor_gates_to_make.htm 

De Morgans Theorem is very good to know too after you understand the above:
http://www.mwit.ac.th/~physicslab/hbase/electronic/demorgan.html 
http://www.allaboutcircuits.com/vol_4/chpt_7/8.html

I have vmware player running Windows 2000 PRO on top of Fedora 9.  I have
Samba 3 running in work group mode and my work group is simply called home.
Strangely enough, I can access a real Windows 2000 box from the virtual one
that is on the same network media as the host system, but that real 2000
system can't access the virtual one.  I can see the virtual one though from
the real one, which is so strange.  I get some kind of path error problem
when I try to click on the virtual machine from the real one in  
network neighborhood.  Interestingly, I can ping back and forth.  The  
gateways in
my network route packets to the host system of the virtual machine just
fine and I can ping just fine from both the virtual machine to the real
one and vice versa.  I don't know how netbios discovers the path between
two machines, but I get the impression that I might need to propagate
the broadcast somehow.

Can a windows workgroup stretch across two network segments?  Namely, the
virtual network on the vmware host and the real one that the host and real
2000 system are on?

Sorry this is so confusing, but it is hard to describe what I'm doing.

Wayne E. Van Loon Sr. wrote:
> Hello All:
> I am working on a project which required writing a driver.
> The hardware is an embedded VersaLogic PC-104 system (ISA bus).
> The processor is a Pentium M, 1.6 GHz with 512 MB memory.
> Operating system is Slackware 12.0 Linux with a 2.6.25.7 kernel compiled 
> with the low latency preemptible kernel.
>
> One of the tasks of the system involves operating a rotary knife device 
> to size a product moving at 1,000 Ft. / Min. ( 200 In. / Sec). Our 
> desired interrupt period for the driver is 62.5 uSec. generated by a 
> counter / timer on one of the ISA I/O boards. At times, the driver's 
> clock interrupt may not run for >1.2 milliseconds. This doesn't happen 
> very often and when it does happen, it has to happen at the wrong time 
> to degrade our product accuracy. But as all of you know, if it can 
> happen, given enough time, it will happen.
>
> How does this latency compare to what some of you may have experienced? 
> Any suggestions for improvement? Incidentally, running without X doesn't 
> improve things. The latencytop utility apparently only works for 
> user-land processes, so it didn't help.
>
> As an aside, I have written a simulator to test the system using 
> real-time Linux. The real-time Linux system is a 1.8 GHz Athlon that is 
> happy running a 31.25 uSec period interrupt with ~ 15 uSec. max latency 
> - roughly a 80x improvement. I usually use real-time Linux for something 
> like this, but this customer didn't like the idea or at least wanted me 
> to give a standard Linux a real good try first.
>
> I plan to be at the PLUG meeting Thursday, so if anyone would like to 
> discuss this application (or buy beer ;-), that would be fun.
> Thanks,
> Wayne
>

> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug

Try specifying the IP address, and verify that you can reach ports 139 and
445 on the virtual 2k instance.


> Can a windows workgroup stretch across two network segments?  Namely, the
> virtual network on the vmware host and the real one that the host and real
> 2000 system are on?
>

What do you mean by segments? Do you have vmware set up in NAT mode? If so,
it won't work, at least not well. If you set it up in bridged mode, it will
likely work better.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sat Jan 17 09:25:50 2009
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sat, 17 Jan 2009 09:25:50 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] 1 TB Hard drives
In-Reply-To: <3AC0BD2385F44FE2BCFC0E4B0E48B3DE@RAMA>
References: <3AC0BD2385F44FE2BCFC0E4B0E48B3DE@RAMA>
Message-ID: <20090117172550.GB10006@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Fri, Jan 16, 2009 at 09:46:19PM -0800, William A Morita wrote:
> I think I know why the 1 TB drives at Fry's are so cheap:
> 
> 	Seagate Hard Drive Fiasco Grows 
> 	http://hardware.slashdot.org/article.pl?sid=09/01/17/0115207

That is quite a few model numbers, but not all of them.  You can
see the list of vulnerable Seagate SATA drives at:

http://seagate.custkb.com/seagate/crm/selfservice/search.jsp?DocId=207931

There are no notebook drives (ST9xxx ) or PATA drives (no trailing S)
on the list.  The good news is that Seagate is very good about 
upporting drives under their 5 year warranty.  The bad news is
that the data may be unrecoverable on a bricked drive. 

Do your backups!

Keith

On Sun, 4 Jan 2009, Ken Stephens wrote:

> Apache server may be your bain.

   After a long session, Kurt discovered the solution. Both
/etc/cups/client.conf and each user's environment need to define
CUPS_SERVER, and both hostnames must be the same.

   I had /etc/cups/client.conf looking at host.domain.tld and ~/.bash_profile
exporting the environment variable as localhost. Same machine, different
names. The only application that noticed the difference and was bothered by
it was SQL-Ledger. Go figure.

From plug_1 at robinson-west.com  Sat Jan 17 16:37:08 2009
From: plug_1 at robinson-west.com (Michael Robinson)
Date: Sat, 17 Jan 2009 16:37:08 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Linux and Freedos...
Message-ID: <1232239028.7610.51.camel@eagle.robinson-west.com>

Linux and Freedos support a web browser called Arachne.  I don't like
Arachne because it has built in pop3 support. I use imap on my network.
Arachne lacks built in filtering.  I also dislike Arachne due to the
fact that it is woefully behind in supporting html and php based web
pages.  Try to access a horde webmail imp site with Arachne, it is
almost as bad as lynx.

Freedos is nearing a 1.1 release.  I've suggested on the freedos 
list that this is a good time to think about the purpose of 
freedos.  I for one am certain that some people who use dos use 
it thinking that it can't browse the web.  Dos in general is the 
most susceptible to viruses and other malware as far as OS'es go, 
or am I wrong?

I've been disappointed in both Linux and Freedos for poor support
of Netware IPX/SPX networking.  MARS netware emulator seems to be
a dead project.  People say, use samba and IP based networking.
That is great, unless you want to keep your network from accessing
the Internet.  IPX/SPX networks without an IP to IPX gateway cannot
go online.  For dos, this would make a lot of sense.  Because dhcp
to my knowledge doesn't have a reasonable way to support different
network configurations based on the OS of the client, IP based
networking where I want to avoid Internet connectivity is a pain.

I've pushed on the freedos list for revival of the MARS netware
emulator, but I'll probably fail to get much traction.

There's no telling when Freedos 1.1 will come out let alone how
much of an improvement it will be over Freedos 1.0.

As far as software goes, what can be done in Freedos that can't
be done in Linux?  I'd like to host an update site on my Linux
server for Freedos, but I don't know that there will be 
instructions on how to do that.

Netware style IPX/SPX networking under Linux seems dead.

Why is the doszilla project dead?  If firefox were dos
compatible, that wouldn't be bad because firefox has an
effective filtering plugin.

On Sat, Jan 17, 2009 at 03:03:11PM -0800, Kyle Terry wrote:
> 
> While on the subject of hard drives, I found this article to be quite
> interesting.
> http://www.techulas.com/tech-news/en/9771.html
> 
> -- 
> Kyle Terry | www.kyleterry.com

Though it claims that Seagate has not acknowledged the problem,
which is not true:

http://seagate.custkb.com/seagate/crm/selfservice/search.jsp?DocId=207931

No date on the Seagate page, which I found today, so perhaps the
tech-news page is merely a little out of date.   Always remember
the two rules of data storage:

1) Shit happens.
  (in Thailand, where the factory is, the phrase is "My Pen Ry")

2) If you don't have two copies, you will end up with zero copies.

The interesting thing will be whether Seagate handles the repairs
and replacements as well as they have in the past.  Fixing this
could suck a lot of cash out of the company during a time when
sales are probably low.  If anyone has good or bad Seagate customer
service experiences to share, I would be interested in hearing
them - if they do well now, they will likely do well in lesser
future crises.  Hint:  don't expect them to recover your data 
from a bricked drive, just a replacement.

> That is great, unless you want to keep your network from accessing
> the Internet.  IPX/SPX networks without an IP to IPX gateway cannot
> go online.  For dos, this would make a lot of sense.  Because dhcp

> network configurations based on the OS of the client, IP based
> networking where I want to avoid Internet connectivity is a pain.
Novell 7 was all IP based and IPX/SPX tunneling was the default method
of using IPX when it was needed to go across a WAN.

>
> I've pushed on the freedos list for revival of the MARS netware
> emulator, but I'll probably fail to get much traction.

The kernel still has the IPX protocol in the networking stack.
The docs in "make config"  references IPX-HOWTO from http://www.tlpd.org
/docs.html#howto
It also mentions a different project called lwared available from
ftp://ibiblio.org/pub/Linux/system/network/daemons/

>....
> Netware style IPX/SPX networking under Linux seems dead.

You might want to check out the SUSE distros as they are being supported
by Novell now.

It says that lwared is unstable.  Whether or not that is true 
anymore is hard to say.  These HOWTO's really need to be brought 
up to date.  I guess I should pop over to http://www.tldp.org/ 
and see if the HOWTO there is any different.  

I'm beginning to think that I'd be better off coming up with 
a dhcp trick to slip my dos computers onto a different network.  
I find I can install Netware server 4.7 student edition to a 
MS DOS 5 system running under vmware, but getting nic drivers 
for it is another story.  Why won't vmware let you choose what 
network card to emulate?

Just popped over to http://www.tldp.org/ and checked the HOWTO's.  
The IPX HOWTO hasn't been updated since 1998.  I guess there 
isn't much interest in IPX networking.

There's still the problem of coming up with IPX packet drivers
for my built in Intel nic.  I was lucky to happen upon a beta
quality TCP/IP dos packet driver distributed by Intel.  When 
I try to grab Netware drivers that match the vmware nic which
are supposed to work in dos, I get a windows executable.

I don't trust Novell, if I recall Novell tried to offer insurance
to people who run Linux claiming that Linux infringes on Microsoft's
and it's own intellectual property or some other such rubbish.

I guess I'm stuck manually configuring a TCP/IP based network
and isolating it.



From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Sun Jan 18 09:19:50 2009
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Sun, 18 Jan 2009 09:19:50 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Cutting the cable - mythtv/mythvodka and online video
In-Reply-To: <bfc61080901171419p5e49737ar6af66cb8de58a082@mail.gmail.com>
References: <bfc61080901171419p5e49737ar6af66cb8de58a082@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20090118171950.GA2418@sentinelchicken.org>


Hi Noj,

I currently use mythtv with no cable or fios or anything of the sort.  I
wanted to slowly transition to DTV in a cheap way, so I dropped the
service and used a coupon to buy a converter box.  I also bought an IR
blaster: 
  http://iguanaworks.net/products.psp

which works pretty well.  It's been slightly buggy here and there, but
they're good with supporting it and it's focused on use under Linux.
You can train it to learn just about any remote control on the receive
side, and then use that same configuration in lirc to send signals on to
converter boxes, cable boxes, etc.

Anyway, this was a transition step for me to make sure the IR blaster
would work without blowing money on cable.  If I feel it's worth it, I
might get some kind of basic digital cable or fios later and use the
blaster to change channels on those boxes.  However, for now the digital
broadcast channels + netflix are keeping us happy.

As for the online video stuff you mentioned, I have no experience with
it.  

Also beware of your converter boxes... many of the companies
manufacturing these are just in existence to make a quick buck off of
the government coupon program and crank out really crappy hardware with
little support.  I bought an Apex Digital box because it was featureful
and reasonably priced, but it died after a few months and their support
is horrendous.  If it's for your parents, consider buying an extra
converter box as a backup or something.

Good luck,
tim

>
> Though it claims that Seagate has not acknowledged the problem,
> which is not true:
>
> http://seagate.custkb.com/seagate/crm/selfservice/search.jsp?DocId=207931
>
> No date on the Seagate page, which I found today, so perhaps the
> tech-news page is merely a little out of date.   Always remember
> the two rules of data storage:
>
> 1) Shit happens.
>  (in Thailand, where the factory is, the phrase is "My Pen Ry")
>
> 2) If you don't have two copies, you will end up with zero copies.
>

There was an interesting Slashdot discussion a few days ago about the
Seagate problem.  My take-away from it was:

1) All hard-drive manufacturers periodically go through rough patches,
so just because people have had good/bad experiences with
Seagate/WD/IBM/etc. doesn't mean others should buy/avoid these brands.

2) Whenever possible, buy drives from different manufactures at
different times.  Problem drives tend to come in batches, so avoid
buying drives in batches.  This lessens the chance you'll end up with
a bunch of problems all at once.

3) Maxtor sucks. :-)

Probably nothing people around here didn't already know, but it was
instructive for me.


Michael M.

On Fri, Jan 16, 2009 at 5:06 PM, Rogan Creswick <creswick at gmail.com> wrote:
> On Fri, Jan 16, 2009 at 1:59 PM, Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com> wrote:
>> I certainly hope not. User home directories ought to be determined via
>> the getpwent() routines, $HOME, or their moral equivalent. Assuming
>> /home/$USER is a recipe for a major disaster. Developers doing such a
>> thing ought to be banished!
>
> Agreed!
>
>> Other than Mac OS X, I've never seen an auto-created $HOME/Documents.
>>
>> Nor have I seen Linux applications that assume the existence of
>> ~/Documents.
>
> Ubuntu 8.10 auto-creates a whole slew of directories, including
> ~/Documents (If I remember correctly: ~/Documents, ~/Music,
> ~/Pictures, ~/Desktop, ~/Public and ~/Videos).
>
> I don't know what makes use of those locations -- they may just be
> there to encourage that organization, but I have a sinking suspicion
> that something does... when I find out what, it'll get a quick trip to
> the bit bucket.

I believe, except perhaps for the ~/Public folder, that the
organization you're seeing is just Ubuntu devs trying to be helpful.
The default audio player on Ubuntu, Rhythmbox, is pre-configured to
look for audio files in ~/Music, so that if you copy audio files
there, Rhythmbox will find them the first time (and all subsequent
times) you launch it.  Of course, you can put your audio files
anywhere, open Rhythmbox and configure it to look anywhere you want.
Having Rhythmbox set up to look in ~/Music and having an auto-created
folder there is just supposed to make it easier for Ubuntu newbies to
get up and running quickly.

The GNOME screensaver will only do a slideshow of images in
~/Pictures, if you choose the "Pictures folder" option.  At least,
there's no way to change it in the GUI -- there may be a gconf way to
change where it looks for images; I've never explored that.  You can
point Xscreensaver to look in any folder from the GUI configuration,
but I'm not sure if you can make it look in multiple folders.

As for ~/Documents, I can imagine the first post-install moment of
confusion many Windows users new to *nix experience is wondering where
"My Documents" are.  Now they know.

>> A separate partition for stuff that you're going to carry through
>> various upgrades is a good idea, but I wouldn't *mount* it under your
>> home directory. I'd mount it as, say, /opt/Documents and then
>> symlink to it from your home directory.
>
> Can you elaborate on why this is preferable to mounting something like
> that in $HOME?

I would love to know that too.

SATA2 Controllers are mostly PCI-E, I'm assuming from your Chipset your
board doesn't have PCI-E, I found the SIIG SATA II-150 PCI which is
relatively inexpensive compared to others. Your transfer rates will be
limited by using PCI versus PCI-E but like you said it's a temporary
solution right?

From alan at clueserver.org  Mon Jan 19 09:36:34 2009
From: alan at clueserver.org (Alan)
Date: Mon, 19 Jan 2009 09:36:34 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] ANNOUNCEMENT PLUG Advanced Topics January 21st 2008
Message-ID: <1232386594.6123.0.camel@rotwang.fnordora.org>

PLUG Advanced Topics Meeting
January 21st, 2008  7pm

Jax Bar
826 SW 2nd Ave
Portland, OR 97204
(503) 228-9128

Speaker:     Eric Wilhelm <scratchcomputing at gmail.com>

Codeless GUI Programming
  A Declarative Syntax Layer for Desktop Graphical User Interfaces

This will be the world premiere of a game-changing advancement in the 
development of desktop graphical user interfaces (GUIs).  FreeTUIT 
removes the verbosity, tedium, and confusion from GUI development and 
provides a unified syntax for widget layout and configuration which 
supports good software design practice without getting in the way of 
rapid application development.

FreeTUIT is a syntax and runtime for concisely declaring the layout and 
configuration of GUI widgets (such as forms, toolbars, buttons, and 
dialogs).  The freetuit interpreter drives a unified object layer which 
is accessible from event callbacks.  This takes you from a blank page 
to a static demo of the layout with zero setup and allows desktop 
applications to be developed and deployed faster than web applications 
by simply removing the HTML, CSS, XML, HTTP, Javascript, Database, 
Network, and User Agent components.

Jax is next to the 2nd Ave Max stop.

> This will be the world premiere of a game-changing advancement in the
> development of desktop graphical user interfaces (GUIs).  FreeTUIT
> removes the verbosity, tedium, and confusion from GUI development and
> provides a unified syntax for widget layout and configuration which
> supports good software design practice without getting in the way of
> rapid application development.
>
> FreeTUIT is a syntax and runtime for concisely declaring the layout and
> configuration of GUI widgets (such as forms, toolbars, buttons, and
> dialogs).  The freetuit interpreter drives a unified object layer which
> is accessible from event callbacks.  This takes you from a blank page
> to a static demo of the layout with zero setup and allows desktop
> applications to be developed and deployed faster than web applications
> by simply removing the HTML, CSS, XML, HTTP, Javascript, Database,
> Network, and User Agent components.
>
> Jax is next to the 2nd Ave Max stop.
>
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug

From qhartman at gmail.com  Mon Jan 19 10:26:38 2009
From: qhartman at gmail.com (Quentin Hartman)
Date: Mon, 19 Jan 2009 10:26:38 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] /home/user partition question
In-Reply-To: <99dafee90901181206h7dc35077k4e6f8f18d9a47a8d@mail.gmail.com>
References: <4970FF48.7090601@comcast.net>
	<alpine.OSX.2.00.0901161352460.32651@guild.galois.com>
	<c834ec80901161706l4725a0cma91e61ec82e609c2@mail.gmail.com>
	<99dafee90901181206h7dc35077k4e6f8f18d9a47a8d@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <8975a95f0901191026g6cd52d39xc4750c0c014decfe@mail.gmail.com>

On Sun, Jan 18, 2009 at 12:06 PM, Michael M. Moore
<michael at writemoore.net> wrote:
>> Can you elaborate on why this is preferable to mounting something like
>> that in $HOME?
>
> I would love to know that too.

My guess is that Paul suggested it simply because that's sort of the
standard convention for doing that sort of thing. It's the "right" way
to do it. If at some point you decided you wanted to do something else
with that space, having it mounted directly in /home/$user would make
accommodating that other use much uglier. So, putting it in /opt,
/mnt, /media, etc and then symlinking makes it a little more flexible.
That's really the only technical advantage I can imagine. However,
since that kind of setup is the convention, following the convention
makes things for discoverable and sensible to someone else who might
be using the machine.

Now, all that said, on a personal machine where you are both the only
regular user and the admin, it doesn't really matter. All of that
convention came about from managing multi-user systems with multiple
admins. On your box, do whatever makes you happy and makes sense to
you.

Myself, I tend to do Paul suggested simply because it is my habit, and
as a result, it makes sense to me.

-QH-

Device      Size   Filesystem   Mount Point

/opt/music, /opt/photos, /opt/backups, and /opt/spare. (I would change 
spare to Documents.) Then, in /home/rsteff, create symlinks to point to 
each of them.

From gently at gmail.com  Mon Jan 19 11:13:12 2009
From: gently at gmail.com (chris (fool) mccraw)
Date: Mon, 19 Jan 2009 11:13:12 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] /home/user partition question
In-Reply-To: <8975a95f0901191026g6cd52d39xc4750c0c014decfe@mail.gmail.com>
References: <4970FF48.7090601@comcast.net>
	<alpine.OSX.2.00.0901161352460.32651@guild.galois.com>
	<c834ec80901161706l4725a0cma91e61ec82e609c2@mail.gmail.com>
	<99dafee90901181206h7dc35077k4e6f8f18d9a47a8d@mail.gmail.com>
	<8975a95f0901191026g6cd52d39xc4750c0c014decfe@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <ae2db4620901191113t42cb1b2eq88f59089cb3fd76f@mail.gmail.com>

> On Sun, Jan 18, 2009 at 12:06 PM, Michael M. Moore
> <michael at writemoore.net> wrote:
>>> Can you elaborate on why this is preferable to mounting something like
>>> that in $HOME?
>>
>> I would love to know that too.


i see a few benefits aside from "it's tradition", but they may not be
relevant to your usage patterns:

1) i've run into a problem with relative-paths in mainly mp3
playlists.  works great on linux, works not so great when exported for
use on windows via samba.  i think it makes more sense to make a
static dir like /opt (i choose /spoo/mp3 for this application) that
works the same as a windows path (winamp at least is smart enough to
trade \ for / in paths, if the path is otherwise the same).

2) i often wonder "what is using up all my space in this directory?"
and run du -sk * to find out.  oops, i caught another partition in my
output if it's mounted somewhere unexpected like /home or /home/fool.

3) i am much more likely to type rm -rf ~/a* then i am to type rm -rf
/<anything>.

4) i occasionally share my files or allow other user login to the
fileserver to browse.  it's useful to be able to point people
somewhere outside of my homedir, which has 15 years worth of crap in
it, with which i am quite comfortable, but which makes navigating it
for those who don't share my brain quite difficult.

5) i have two user accounts on most of my machines--one for my typical
barebones setup (yay mouseless computing with ratpoison(1)) and
another that fires up whatever desktop environment the distro ships
with.  they necessarily have different homedirs (same uid, though) so
that they don't share config files.  getting used to ~/asdf in one and
/home/fool/asdf in the other is annoying.

there are workarounds for every one of those things, i know.  but hey,
why bother when i get the benefits for free just by mounting it
somewhere else?


From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Mon Jan 19 11:46:16 2009
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Mon, 19 Jan 2009 11:46:16 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] /home/user partition question
In-Reply-To: <4974CDD5.3090500@comcast.net>
References: <4970FF48.7090601@comcast.net>
	<8975a95f0901191026g6cd52d39xc4750c0c014decfe@mail.gmail.com>
	<4974CDD5.3090500@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <200901191146.16132.scratchcomputing@gmail.com>

# from Richard C. Steffens
# on Monday 19 January 2009 11:00:

>If I understand correctly, the "right way" is to mount sdb1-4 as
>/opt/music, /opt/photos, /opt/backups, and /opt/spare. (I would change
>spare to Documents.) Then, in /home/rsteff, create symlinks to point
> to each of them.

I think using /opt is discouraged by the fhs - or at least it is spec'd 
as 'add-on application packages', but then /mnt is supposed to be "a 
temporarily mounted filesystem".  And of course, the fhs doesn't 
concern itself with user data so much as 'application data'.

I've taken to mounting disks which act as "a place where I keep $stuff" 
as /disks/$stuff and then loop mounting into /srv/$stuff.  Similarly, 
any "just a big disk" is e.g. /disks/space.

From there, you can bind mount the whole disk or a subdirectory 
to /srv/nfs/music and so on.

Symlinks can sometimes be problematic, but might be the best way to go 
for making paths appear in your home directory.

--Eric

Introducing change is like pulling off a bandage: the pain is a memory
almost as soon as you feel it.
--Paul Graham

(3rd attempt at posting this; guess mailman is picky)

I recently opted to try Clear.com's WiMAX Home service [1] as a replacement
for DSL.  The Home service consists of a Motorola CPEi150 [2] bridge(?)
device that connects the Clear's WiMAX transmitter and does NAT and DHCP
and some basic firewalling.  It's a dynamically assigned IP unless one
shells out an extra $10/month for a static IP.

Anyways, while basic web surfing seems to work fine, ssh sessions are
either disconnecting or just hanging frequently, usually within 5-10
minutes.  Doesn't seem to matter if I'm actively typing or just running
something like top(1).  I tried adding ServerAliveInterval <sec> with
various time intervals (180, 240, 300) to my ssh_config file without much
improvement.  It's pretty annoying and I'm almost to the point of boxing
it up.  Anyone have any insight as to what might be the problem?  An online
chat with Clear's support service wasn't particularly enlightening; the
tech thought perhaps packet splitting might be the cause.  My NIC is
currently set at MTU=1500 - pretty standard I think.

I'm about 5 days into the 7 day evaluation period, after which I'm locked
into Clear's two year WiMAX contract, so any timely feedback would be
greatly appreciated.  Off-list replies are encouraged as I'm subscribed
to the list's digest.

Thanks,
Jeff

on my laptop with this command:

sudo mount -t nfs 192.168.0.148:/media/md3 /media/Devil6_md3/

and /media/Devil6_md3/ is the mount point on the laptop. There are two
problems with this that I'd like to resolve:

1) Every time I mount the desktop partition on the laptop I must first
figure out what the desktop address has become. For example, the last
time I did this its address was 192.168.0.126. Today it is
192.168.0.148. It is easy enough to use the GUI on the desktop to
figure it out (System > Adminsitration > Network Tools, then click on
the ethernet device in the dropdown). But I shouldn't have to do this.
Is there a way to make things stay the same?

2) (More critical). Today it won't let me mount it:

jjj at Devil7:~$ sudo mount -t nfs
192.168.0.148:/media/md3 /media/Devil6_md3/ 
mount.nfs: access denied by server while mounting
192.168.0.148:/media/md3

I never got denied access before. What happened? How can I give myself
permission? And while I'm at it, it would be more convenient to GUI-ize
this. Perhaps a script that I can make executable and set up with an
icon in the panel? Something like:

!/bin/bash
sudo mount -t nfs 192.168.0.148:/media/md3 /media/Devil6_md3/

From wevl at pacifier.com  Mon Jan 19 13:07:52 2009

John:
I don't know if you will think this is any better or not.

I always set my machines up with static IP addressing. When they boot, 
they always have the same address. If I want to go on line via my 
ethernet DSL MODEM and router, as root "/sbin/dhcpcd" gets an address 
from the router.

When done on line, as root, I reset my IP address back to my static 
address with "ifconfig eth0 192.168.1.xxx up". I can then nfs mount, 
ssh, telnet etc. using the hostname.
Wayne

Usually, I have no problem setting static IP addresses up if they're
on the same subnet as my router, but outside of the DHCP pool. For
example, my router is set to hand out addresses 192.168.19.64/24 to
192.168.19.96/24. If I set my system to 192.168.19.4/24, it can get
out to the Internet, AND I have a static IP address for it.

>> jjj at Devil7:~$ sudo mount -t nfs
>> 192.168.0.148:/media/md3 /media/Devil6_md3/
>> mount.nfs: access denied by server while mounting
>> 192.168.0.148:/media/md3
>>
>> I never got denied access before. What happened? How can I give myself
>> permission? And while I'm at it, it would be more convenient to GUI-ize

In my very brief test, I had multiple instances where my client device
(a laptop connected by ethernet) had to manually release/renew my dhcp
lease on the LAN side of the WiMax device.  In your shoes my

satisfied.  Once the evaluation period expires, you have no leverage.
If you are prepared to walk away, you are likely to have their maximum
attention.

John> 1) Every time I mount the desktop partition on the laptop I must
John> first figure out what the desktop address has become. For
John> example, the last time I did this its address was
John> 192.168.0.126. Today it is 192.168.0.148. It is easy enough to

John> device in the dropdown). But I shouldn't have to do this.  Is

Two choices:

 a) static network configuration of your client devices rather than
    using dhcp; or 

 b) configuring your dhcp server to always hand out the same ip addrs
    by mac address.

> example, my router is set to hand out addresses 192.168.19.64/24 to

> out to the Internet, AND I have a static IP address for it.

> >> jjj at Devil7:~$ sudo mount -t nfs
> >> 192.168.0.148:/media/md3 /media/Devil6_md3/
> >> mount.nfs: access denied by server while mounting
> >> 192.168.0.148:/media/md3
> >>
> >> I never got denied access before. What happened? How can I give myself
> >> permission? And while I'm at it, it would be more convenient to GUI-ize
> >> this. Perhaps a script that I can make executable and set up with an
> >> icon in the panel? Something like:
> 
> Let's see your /etc/exports file.

For the laptop:
---------
#		to NFS clients.  See exports(5).
#
# Example for NFSv2 and NFSv3:
# /srv/homes       hostname1(rw,sync) hostname2(ro,sync)
#
# Example for NFSv4:
# /srv/nfs4        gss/krb5i(rw,sync,fsid=0,crossmnt)
# /srv/nfs4/homes  gss/krb5i(rw,sync)
#
#/ Devil6(rw)
/               192.168.0.101(rw)
--------

Note that the desktop is Devil6 and the laptop is Devil7. 

The GUI on the desktop displays the hardware address of the ethernet
controller. It also shows "ethernet interface (eth1) as IPv4:
192.168.0.148."

On the laptop the GUI displays the hardware address of its ethernet
controller as well, and its IPv4 address as 192.168.0.101.

I don't know offhand what the router's address is. I can't find the
documentation either. But it's a Netgear that's only a year old, so no
doubt I could get the documentation from Netgear online and then figure
out how to determine its address.

Looking at the /etc/exports file on the desktop reveals what may be the
problem. Its last two lines are:

/media/md3	Devil7(rw) 192.168.0.102(rw)
/			Devil7(rw)

It looks to me as though the desktop is willing to give permission to
Devil 7 but only if its address is 192.168.0.102. So I manually changed
the /etc/exports file on the desktop to 192.168.0.101 and tried again
the mount the partition on the laptop. Still got an access denied
message, although the text of the messae is slightly different:

jjj at Devil7:~$ sudo mount -t nfs 192.168.0.148:/media/md3 /media/Devil6_md3/ 
mount.nfs: access denied by server while mounting 192.168.0.148:/media/md3

From mike at mommabears.com  Mon Jan 19 16:22:25 2009
From: mike at mommabears.com (MJang)
Date: Mon, 19 Jan 2009 16:22:25 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Dumbass network question
In-Reply-To: <20090119120418.a59a3d0e.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20090119120418.a59a3d0e.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <1232410945.7685.23.camel@UbuntuHH>

Dear John, 

In general, one way to avoid problems like you suggest is:

1) Static IP addresses (or consistent IP address assignments as Russell
suggests)
2) NFS sharing for the entire network. 

You've listed the following in your /etc/exports


/               192.168.0.101(rw)
--------
/media/md3	Devil7(rw) 192.168.0.102(rw)
/			Devil7(rw)

In general, I'd avoid sharing with hostnames and single IP address on a
private network, unless security within your network is at issue. (And
it's also risky to share the top-level root directory (/).)

So you might change your /etc/exports to

/              192.168.0.0/255.255.255.0(rw)

/media/md3     192.168.0.0/255.255.255.0(rw)

Though I'd encourage you to focus the sharing on more regular directory
like /home.

Thanks,
Mike

I just looked up and there are different models, but the first one listed by
google was the DGL-4100. In the manual for that, under DHCP there's a
section for "Add Static DHCP Client" which then lets you enter the MAC
address and tell it which IP address that MAC address should get every time
it requests one. This is on page 19 of the pdf manual from this page:
http://games.dlink.com/products/?pid=371&#DGL-4100
Direct link:
ftp://ftp.dlink.com/Gateway/dgl4100/Manual/dgl4100_manual_100.zip

I would expect other models to at least be similar.

Good luck,
Erik

Another option would be ftp or sftp.  Since I have only two machines
and haven't really had the need for a complex (or even simple) local
network, I just use a simple ftp client to move files back and forth.
Debian/Ubuntu have several graphical and command-line ftp clients to
pick from.

I guess it would get tedious if you need to move lots and lots of
files from one machine to another all the time.  But if you're only
doing a handful of files a day, it's quick and easy.

Networking is ... mind-boggling.

>
> I think using /opt is discouraged by the fhs - or at least it is spec'd
> as 'add-on application packages', but then /mnt is supposed to be "a
> temporarily mounted filesystem".  And of course, the fhs doesn't
> concern itself with user data so much as 'application data'.

Quite some time ago, Arch Linux used /opt for Mozilla apps (Firefox,
SeaMonkey, etc.) and, IIRC, OpenOffice.org.  I was never quite clear
why, and I don't think Arch does that anymore.  It was irritating
because /opt wasn't in a user's path by default and at the time, I
wasn't exactly sure what a path was, how it worked, or how to modify
it.  Debian doesn't seem to use /opt for anything, at least nothing I
have installed.  It's empty.

>
> I've taken to mounting disks which act as "a place where I keep $stuff"
> as /disks/$stuff and then loop mounting into /srv/$stuff.  Similarly,
> any "just a big disk" is e.g. /disks/space.

I keep a "stuff" partition on a second hard drive, but set it up to
automount at boot time under root.  A while ago I asked if there were
any security implications in having a directory mounted under / but
owned by a user & user's group, and no one seemed to think this was a
problem.  I haven't had the kind of relative-path issues Chris
mentioned because I pretty mostly use it for excess stuff I haven't
sorted and pruned.  I tend to think of it as my procrastination
partition.  Once in a while when I'm dual-booting, I might put a few
things there that I want to access from both OS'es, so I don't have to
mount the home partition from the other OS.

> on the laptop. Or maybe I can specify my username and password for the
> desktop when issuing the mount command on the laptop. I can't find any
> instructions on this in man mount.

Dear John, 

FWIW, NFS doesn't authenticate mounts based on usernames or passwords.
I've tried what I think is equivalent to your configuration, with the
following in my /etc/exports:

/ 192.168.0.0/255.255.255.0(rw)

I think /etc/exports is pretty sensitive to things like extra spaces. If
I remember right, even a space after the end of the line could lead to
errors. 

If the share is properly exported, you should be able to read it with a
showmount -e command. For example, if the NFS server is on
192.168.0.148, you'd run the following command, and get the noted export
list:

$ showmount -e 192.168.0.148
Export list for 192.168.0.148:
/                     192.168.0.0/255.255.255.0

After I confirm that works, I run the following command, which works:

$ sudo mount -t nfs 192.168.0.148:/ /mnt/

To confirm, I find the following in my /var/log/syslog file:
Jan 20 11:02:19 ubuntuhardy3 mountd[6807]: authenticated mount request
from 192.168.0.8:776 for / (/)

I also get the following in the output to the mount command:

192.168.0.148:/ on /mnt type nfs (rw,addr=192.168.0.148)


Thanks,
Mike



From keithl at kl-ic.com  Tue Jan 20 13:38:10 2009
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Tue, 20 Jan 2009 13:38:10 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] question: low power CAT5 switches
Message-ID: <20090120213810.GB26429@gate.kl-ic.com>

I spent way too much time yesterday "rationalizing" a 15 year 
accumulation of cat5 and coax ethernet cables in my office and
the house (among other things).   Now my ALIX firewall/router
and 2 out of 6 ethernet switches are stacked together in one
place (talking to 3 other switches and up to 7 other computers), 
cables are shorter and labelled at the ends, unused cables are
eliminated, etc.  After all the work optimizing power on the 4
watt ALIX, the power drawn by the 8 port Linksys EZXS88W switches
are now the most significant power usage in the network - they
draw around 6W each, idle.

In theory, an ideal 802.3 ethernet PHY driver uses around 80mW
when transmitting a packet (2.8V into 100 ohms) and nothing 
when idle.  The high signalling voltages are legacy, from the
days when ethernet and analog telephones shared cable bundles -
an analog phone going on or off hook can inject a 250mV crosstalk
spike into the ethernet pairs.

A 100% active 8 port switch transmitting on half its ports
should ideally consume less than 0.4W peak, much less idling.
Of course, the internals will take some power, but modern
electronics consumes less and less per digital function than
the old stuff.

There is a new standard coming, 802.3az energy efficient
ethernet, that will reduce the power still more in data
centers.  That probably will not interoperate with existing
gear, so it is not a consideration for me.  Still, it should
be possible to reduce the power a lot for plain old 802.3.

So, I would like to find new switches (4, 8, and 16 port)
that reduce the power.  Suggestions?

Keith


-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

Subject: [PLUG] Seagate ST3500320AS (was Re:  1 TB Hard drives)
In-Reply-To: <20090117172550.GB10006@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <3AC0BD2385F44FE2BCFC0E4B0E48B3DE@RAMA>
	<20090117172550.GB10006@gate.kl-ic.com>

>>>>> "Keith" == Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com> writes:

Bill> Seagate Hard Drive Fiasco Grows
Bill> http://hardware.slashdot.org/article.pl?sid=09/01/17/0115207

Keith> That is quite a few model numbers, but not all of them.  You
Keith> can see the list of vulnerable Seagate SATA drives at:

Keith> http://seagate.custkb.com/seagate/crm/selfservice/search.jsp?DocId=207931
For the latest twist in this, see: 

  Seagate's Update Bricking Working HDDs
  http://www.tomshardware.com/news/seagate-500gb-1tb-firmware-update,6867.html

Turns out I went and did a survey of the drives I have deployed last
night and discovered that yesterday Seagate released a firmware
upgrade that they recommended be applied to the affected disk models.
Even though my drives were working fine, the specter of sudden disk
failure loomed, and now I'd been warned by the manufacturer.  I
downloaded the small .iso, booted off the FreeDOS image and applied
the (again, i repeat, *recommended*) upgrade.  Boom.  It bricked the
drive.  BIOS sees it, but attempts to interact with it at boot result
in a "Hard Disk Error" message.  Apparently, the firmware SD1A is
busted for the ST3500320AS.

Now, I didn't lose anything of value, other than the work to install.
Turns out a few hours later last night, Seagate withdrew their update
to do more testing/validation.  I reached someone today and was told
that they were working on a new revision and testing.  The said it
might be a week before it's available.  In the meantime, my machine is
dead-ish (about to boot a live CD).  Thanks Seagate.  Heck of a job.

Apparently, this particular behavior is specific to the 500G model
(ST3500320AS), but it certainly raising understandable questions about
the rigor and competence of Seagates firmware engineering and quality
control.

If you downloaded a Seagate harddisk firmware update yesterday and
haven't applied it yet, for your own sake I urge you to *REVIEW
CAREFULLY* the current status of that update and make sure you've got
a convenient copy of the data on the disk that would be updated.  You
update might be sending it on an extended vacation.

Good luck!
